[1] Chapter 1: The Surprise
[2] Bobby Jones placed the ball on the tee, then quickly swung the club a few times in preparation, before slowly lifting the club back and swinging it down with lightning speed.
[3] Would the ball fly straight down the fairway, soaring high over the sand traps, and land on the 14th green where a five-iron shot could easily sink it into the hole?
[4] No, it was a poor top shot that skimmed the ground, landing squarely in the sand trap!
[5] There were no disappointed spectators around to sigh, and the only witness to this shot showed no surprise. This was easily explained, as the golfer who hit this shot was not a native-born American golf master, but Bobby Jones, the fourth son of a Welsh seaside town (Machynlleth) parish priest.
[6] Bobby suddenly blurted out a curse.
[7] He was a friendly young man of about 28 years old. His best friends wouldn't call him handsome, but his face was likable, with brown eyes that had a friendly, honest look, like a dog's.
[8] "I'm getting worse and worse," he muttered dejectedly.
[9] "You hit that long shot too hard," his companion said.
[10] Dr. Thomas was a middle-aged man with gray hair and a ruddy complexion. He never used a high swing himself. He hit the ball along the fairway with a short, straight rod technique and often beat more skilled but less consistent opponents.
[11] Bobbie hit the ball hard with a nine iron, and this time it was successful. The ball stopped near where Dr. Thomas had reached the green with two well-executed iron shots.
[12] "That's your hole," Bobbie said.
[13] They moved on to the next tee.
[14] Dr. Thomas teed off first – a pretty straight shot, but not very far.
[15] Bobbie sighed, placed the ball on the tee, and repositioned it, then pre-swung the club for a long time before stiffly swinging it back, closing his eyes, lifting his head, lowering his right shoulder, and doing everything he didn't need to do, ultimately hitting a long shot down the fairway.
[16] He breathed a satisfied sigh, and the worried expression of a golfer disappeared from his expressive face, replaced by the joy of a well-hit shot.
[17] "Now I know what I've been doing," Bob said somewhat insincerely.
[18] A perfect shot, a small low fly with a five iron, put Bob's ball close to the hole. He made a birdie on his fourth shot, reducing Dr. Thomas's lead to just one stroke.
[19] Bob confidently stepped onto the tee of the 16th hole, doing all the unnecessary motions again, and this time the miracle did not happen. A remarkable, unparalleled, and awe-inspiring right-to-right shot was born! The ball flew off to the right at a right angle.
[20] "If that shot had been straight - oh!" Dr. Thomas said.
[21] "If only...," Bobbie grumbled, "I think I heard someone shout! I hope it didn't hit anyone."
[22] He looked to the right, the light was dimming as the sun was setting, and it was difficult to see anything clearly from that distance. A thin mist was rising from the sea, and the cliff's edge was hundreds of yards away.
[23] "The path goes that way," Bobbie said, "but the ball couldn't have gone that far. I still think I heard a shout. Did you hear it?"
[24] The doctor hadn't heard anything.
[25] Bobbie searched for the hit ball, and after some trouble, he finally found it. It was unplayable, embedded in a thorn bush. He broke some branches, retrieved the ball, and loudly informed his partner that he was giving up the hole.
[26] The doctor approached him, as the next tee was right on the cliff's edge.
[27] The seventeenth hole made Bobbie particularly nervous. You had to hit the ball over a chasm on this hole. The distance wasn't too far, but the gravitational pull of the abyss below was hard to resist.
[28] They walked along the narrow path that hugged the cliff's edge, which turned to their left, extending away from the cliff.
[29] The doctor used an iron-headed club, and the ball landed on the other side.
[30] Bobbie took a deep breath, and swung hard. The ball flew forward, disappearing over the edge of the chasm.
[31] "Always like this," Bobby muttered, "I keep doing the same damn thing."
[32] He walked to the edge of the ravine and looked down. Far below was a shimmering sea, but not all balls would fall that deep. The slope at the top was steep, but it gradually flattened out as it went down.
[33] Bobby walked along the ridge slowly. He knew there was an easy place to climb down. Caddies would do it, jumping over the cliff's edge and appearing later, panting and triumphant, holding the ball they had lost.
[34] Suddenly, Bobby stiffened and called out to his partner.
[35] "Hey, doctor, come over here, take a look at this."
[36] About forty feet below, there was a dark pile of something that looked like a heap of old clothes.
[37] The doctor held his breath.
[38] "Oh, my God," he said. "Someone fell off the cliff. We've got to go down and check it out."
[39] With Bobbie's help, the two of them descended the rock face. They finally arrived at the pile of dark, ominous-looking objects. It was a man in his forties, unconscious but still breathing.
[40] The doctor examined the man, feeling his limbs, checking his pulse, and closing his eyelids. After completing the check while kneeling beside him, he looked up at Bobbie, who was standing nearby and feeling nauseous, and slowly shook his head.
[41] "No hope," he said, "this poor guy is almost gone. He broke his spine. What a shame. I guess he wasn't very familiar with this path, and he stepped off the cliff when it was misty. I've mentioned it to the council more than once that we should add a fence here."
[42] He stood up again.
[43] "I'm going to get some help," he said, "to arrange to get him up. Otherwise, we won't know where we are when it gets dark. Can you stay here?"
[44] Bobbie nodded.
[45] "Can't we help him at all?" he asked.
[46] The doctor shook his head.
[47] "There's nothing we can do. He's going to die soon. His pulse is weakening rapidly, and he'll only last another twenty minutes or so. He may regain consciousness before he dies, but it's just as likely he won't. Still—"
[48] "Of course," Bobby said quickly. "I'll stay here. You go. If he does wake up, is there any medicine or—" He was still uncertain.
[49] The doctor shook his head.
[50] "There won't be any pain," he said. "None at all."
[51] With that, he turned and began to climb the cliff again, swiftly. Bobbie watched him until he waved and disappeared over the cliff top.
[52] Bobbie moved a few steps along the narrow ridge, sitting down on a protruding rock. The events that had just occurred left him stunned. He had never dealt with illness or death before.
[53] What a terrible coincidence! A clear evening, and then this fog, and one misstep and it's all over. The man was good-looking and seemed healthy, never having suffered from a single illness in his life. The pallor of death could not hide his tanned skin. He must have spent a long time outdoors, perhaps overseas. Bobbie looked at him more closely: reddish-brown curls beginning to turn gray at the temples, a broad nose, a square jaw, a mouth with a hint of a smile, revealing a set of white teeth. His shoulders were broad, his hands strong and muscular. His legs were twisted in an odd angle. Bobbie shuddered, looking again at the man's face. It was a handsome face, witty, determined, and resourceful. Those eyes, he thought, must be blue –
[54] Just as he thought this, the eyes suddenly opened.
[55] They were indeed blue, a clear, deep blue. The eyes looked straight at Bobbie, without a hint of hesitation or confusion, appearing completely awake. The eyes were alert, and at the same time, they seemed to want to ask a question.
[56] Bob quickly got up and walked towards the man. Before he reached him, the man spoke. He spoke clearly and loudly, not weakly.
[57] "Why didn't they find Evans?" he said.
[58] Then a strange shiver ran through his body, his eyelids drooped, and his jaw relaxed...
[59] The man was dead.
[60] * * *
[61] In golf, a birdie refers to a hole where the number of strokes used is one less than the standard par.
[62] The European genus of thorny shrubs known as the genus Cytisus.
[63] Chapter 2: Father
[64] Bobbi knelt beside him, and there was no doubt that the man was dead. The sudden question, the last-minute resurgence, and then... nothing. Just death.
[65] With a slight sense of apology, Bobbi reached into the dead man's pocket and pulled out a silk handkerchief. He respectfully spread it out and placed it over the man's face. There was nothing more he could do.
[66] He then noticed that his actions had brought out another item from the dead man's pocket. It was a photograph, which Bobby glanced at before putting it back.
[67] It was a woman's face. Incredibly, it lingered in his mind. It was a beautiful woman, with eyes that were wide apart. She looked young, probably not yet thirty, but it was not her beauty that captivated him, but the power of her beauty to linger in his mind. He thought it was a face he would never forget.
[68] He respectfully placed the photograph back in its original pocket and sat down again, waiting for the doctor to return.
[69] Time passed slowly, at least for the young man waiting. He remembered something else. He had promised his father to play the organ at the six o'clock evening prayer, and it was already ten minutes to six. Of course, his father would understand, but he wished he had asked the doctor to send a message back. Thomas Jones, the vicar, was an extremely neurotic man, prone to making a fuss over nothing, and when he did, his digestive system would suffer, causing him great pain. Although in Bobby's eyes, his father was a pitiful old man, he still liked him very much. On the other hand, Thomas the vicar saw his fourth son as a pitiful little fool, and he was less patient in educating Bobby than Bobby was with himself.
[70] "Poor old dad," thought Bobby, "he's going to be all over the place. He won't know whether to start the evening prayer or not. He'll get all worked up until he feels his stomach ache, and then he won't be able to eat dinner. He doesn't understand that I won't let him down unless it's absolutely unavoidable. And besides, it's just playing the organ, what difference does it make if I don't go? However, he will never see it that way. Anyone over fifty becomes unreasonable and worries about trivial matters. I guess they were raised with completely wrong ideas and can't be corrected now. Poor old dad, his knowledge is no better than that of a young chick!"
[71] He sat there thinking about his father, with mixed feelings. To him, life at home was like a long sacrifice, constantly catering to his father's strange whims. For his father, however, the long sacrifice was actually on his side, misunderstood and misinterpreted by his younger ones. So, the two of them might have very different views on the same issue.
[72] How long had the doctor been gone? He should be back by now, shouldn't he?
[73] Bobby stood up, stamping his foot in annoyance. Just then, he heard some noise upstairs and looked up, relieved that help was on the way and that his task would soon be done.
[74] But it was not the doctor who came, but a man in golf pants that Bobby did not recognize.
[75] "Say," the newcomer said, "what's going on? Is there an accident? Can I help?"
[76] He was tall and spoke in a baritone voice that was pleasing to the ear. As night fell quickly, Bobbie couldn't see his face too clearly.
[77] He explained the situation while the stranger expressed his shock.
[78] "Is there nothing I can do?" he asked, "Find someone to help?"
[79] Bobbie explained that help was on the way and asked the stranger if he could keep an eye out for any signs of the person coming.
[80] "There's still no sign of them yet."
[81] "Listen," Bobbie continues, "I have an appointment at six."
[82] "And you don't want to leave—"
[83] "No, I don't," Bobbie says, "I mean, the poor guy is dead, and of course, we can't do anything, but still—"
[84] He stops, finding it difficult to express his jumbled thoughts, as usual.
[85] The other person seems to understand.
[86] "I get it," he said, "Listen, I can go down – if I can get down – and wait here for them."
[87] "Oh, can you?" Bobby said gratefully, "You know, the person I'm meeting is my father. He's not a bad guy, just prone to getting flustered. Can you see the path? Lean a little to the left – now to the right – that's it. It's not too hard to walk."
[88] He guided the man forward, until the two of them stood face to face on the narrow platform. The man was about thirty-five years old, with a somewhat indecisive face that seemed to belong to a man with a pair of spectacles and a small mustache.
[89] "I'm still new here," he explained, "By the way, my name is Basil Frenchie. I'm looking at a house. Oh, he was so unfortunate! Did he fall off the cliff edge?"
[90] Bobby nodded.
[91] "It's a bit foggy," he explained, "this path is a bit dangerous. Well, see you later. Thank you very much. I have to hurry, you're too kind."
[92] "Don't mention it," the other man said firmly, "anyone would have done the same. It wouldn't be right to leave this poor fellow lying here."
[93] Bobby climbed the steep path. At the top, he waved to the other man and ran off, sprinting across the fields. To save time, he didn't go around to the churchyard gate on the street, but jumped over the wall of the courtyard. This scene was witnessed by the parish priest from the window of the church, who was extremely displeased with it.
[94] It was already five minutes past six, but the clock was still ringing.
[95] All explanations and accusations were postponed until after evening prayer. Bobby sat down in his seat, deftly playing the old organ. His pent-up emotions made his fingers play Chopin's Funeral March.
[96] After evening prayers, the vicar began to reprimand his son with more sorrow than anger (which he particularly emphasized).
[97] "My dear Bob," he said, "if you can't do something properly, then you'd better not do it at all. I understand that you and your young friends seem to have no sense of time, but we shouldn't keep God waiting. It was you who volunteered to play the organ. I didn't force you. And yet, you ran off to play -"
[98] Bob decided to interrupt his father before he started on a long speech.
[99] "Sorry, Dad," he began in a light-hearted tone, as he always did when changing the subject, "it wasn't my fault this time. I was busy with a corpse."
[100] "What are you doing?"
[101] "Standing by a guy who stepped off a cliff. You know, right next to the tee box of the 17th hole, near the rift valley. There was some fog, so he must have just walked right off and fell down the cliff."
[102] "Oh my goodness," the pastor exclaimed, "How terrible! Did he die on the spot?"
[103] "No. He was just unconscious. He died as soon as Dr. Thomas left. And I felt like I had to stay there, I couldn't just leave him and walk away. Then another person came and I passed on the responsibility of watching over him, and I ran back as fast as I could."
[104] The pastor sighed.
[105] "Oh, my dear Bobbie," he said, "is there nothing that can shake you out of your pitiful apathy? This is something that is beyond words for me. You faced death, or a sudden death, and yet you can still make jokes about it! You are indifferent. Everything, everything, no matter how solemn, how sacred, is just a joke to you and your generation."
[106] Bobby shifted his foot.
[107] Well, if his father can't understand that making a joke about something is because you're upset about it, there's nothing to be done. He really doesn't get it! It's not something you can explain. When death and tragedy are right in front of you, you just have to grit your teeth and face it bravely.
[108] But what can you expect? Once you're over fifty, you don't understand anything anymore, your mind is full of the most bizarre notions.
[109] "I reckon it's the war," he thought sincerely. "The war has made them depressed, agitated, and then they're never quite right again."
[110] He felt both ashamed and sorry for his father.
[111] "Sorry, Dad," he said, knowing that an explanation wouldn't help.
[112] The priest felt sorry for his son too – he looked a bit embarrassed – but he was ashamed for him. The boy had no concept of the seriousness of life, and even his apology came across as cheerful and unrepentant.
[113] They walked to the priest's residence, both trying to find excuses for the other in their minds.
[114] The priest thought, "I don't know when Bob will find something to do…"
[115] Bob thought, "I don't know how long I can keep going here…"
[116] However, both of them still deeply loved each other.
[117] Chapter 3: The Railway Journey
[118] Bobby's adventure didn't have any further developments. The next morning, he went into town to see a friend who planned to open an auto repair shop and wanted Bobby to join him, which would be a great help.
[119] After spending two days arranging things, Bobby caught the 11:30 train home. He indeed caught it, but only just barely. When he arrived at Paddington Station, the clock pointed to 11:28, and he rushed into the underground passage, appearing on Platform 3 just as the train started, completely ignoring the angry ticket inspector and porters behind him.
[120] He pulled the carriage door open and tumbled inside, getting up again. The door was slammed shut by a nimble porter, and Bobby found himself facing the only passenger in the carriage.
[121] This was a first-class compartment, and in the corner facing the front of the train sat a dark-skinned girl smoking. She wore a red skirt, a green short jacket, and a bright blue beret. Although she looked somewhat like a monkey beside a street performer's hand organ (she had narrow, sad black eyes and a wrinkled face), she was still quite attractive.
[122] Bobby's apology was on the tip of his tongue when he suddenly stopped.
[123] "Hey, it's you, Frankie!" he said. "It's been a while."
[124] "Yeah, it has. Sit down and chat."
[125] Bobby grinned.
[126] "My ticket color is wrong."
[127] "No worries," Frankie said kindly, "I'll cover the difference."
[128] "My machismo won't allow that," Bobbie said, "How can a lady pay for me?"
[129] "The closest lady seems to have that role," Frankie said.
[130] "I'll make up the difference myself," Bobbie said with a heroic tone, just as a tall, blue figure appeared at the doorway leading to the corridor.
[131] "Leave it to me," Frankie said.
[132] She smiled gracefully at the ticket collector, who took the white card from her hand and punched it while tipping his cap to her.
[133] "Mr. Jones just came in and wanted to speak to me," she said, "Is that alright?"
[134] "No worries, miss. I don't think this gentleman will be here long," he said with a subtle cough, "I won't be back until we reach Bristol."
[135] "A smile can make such a big difference," Bobby said after the ticket collector left.
[136] Miss Francis DeWinter[1] shook her head thoughtfully.
[137] "I wouldn't attribute it to a smile," she said. "I think it's more due to my father's habit of giving everyone a shilling whenever he travels."
[138] "I thought you had given up Wales for good, Frankie," said Francis.
[139] Francis sighed.
[140] "Darling, you know how parents can be. Plus, the state of the bathroom. There's nothing to do, nowhere to go—people just don't want to come out to the countryside anymore. They say they're saving money, that they can't afford to go so far. Oh, I don't know. What am I supposed to do?"
[141] Bobby shook his head, sadly realizing the problem.
[142] "But anyway," Frankie went on, "after last night's party, I felt like our house couldn't be any worse."
[143] "What happened at the party?"
[144] "Nothing. Just like all the other parties, only it was more like a party. It was supposed to start at 8:30 at the Savoy Hotel. Some of us didn't get there until almost 9:15, of course, we ran into other people and it was nearly 10:00 before we shook them off. Then we had dinner, and a little later, we went to 'Puppets on Strings.' There was a rumor that it was going to be raided, but nothing happened, it was just dead. We had a few drinks and then went to 'The Bullring,' which was even more dead, and then we went to a coffee shop, and then to a fish and chips place, and then we wanted to go see if Angela's uncle would be shocked if we showed up for breakfast, but he wasn't, he just thought we were annoying. Then we kind of lost our energy, so we all went home. Honestly, Bobby, it wasn't much fun."
[145] "I didn't think so," Bobby said, trying to hide his envy.
[146] Even in the most wild times, he never dreamed of becoming a member of 'Puppet Show' or 'Bullring.'
[147] Their relationship was somewhat peculiar.
[148] As children, he and his brothers used to play with the children who lived in the castle. As they grew up, they found it hard to meet each other. Whenever they did, they would still call each other by their Christian names. Frankie occasionally stayed at home, and Bob and his brothers would come over to play tennis. However, Frankie and her two brothers were never invited to the parsonage. Everyone knew that they might not find the parsonage interesting. On the other hand, playing tennis always required more people. Although they called each other by their Christian names, they still felt a little awkward. The DeWint family's friendliness seemed to be a bit excessive, as if to say, "We're really not so different." The Jones family, on the other hand, was too formal and stiff, and seemed determined not to accept any more friendship than they already had. Now, aside from some childhood memories, there was nothing in common between the two families. However, Bob still liked Frankie, and he was always happy when fate brought them together unexpectedly.
[149] "I'm tired of all this," Frankie's voice was filled with exhaustion. "Don't you feel the same way?"
[150] Bob thought for a moment.
[151] "No, I don't think so."
[152] "That's good," Frankie said.
[153] "I'm not saying I'm enthusiastic," Bobby said, worried that his words might hurt the other person. "I can't stand overly enthusiastic people."
[154] "I know," Frankie whispered. "They're too terrible."
[155] They looked at each other sympathetically.
[156] "By the way," Franky suddenly said, "what about the man who fell off the cliff?"
[157] "Thomas and I found him," Bobbie said, "How did you know, Franky?"
[158] "I saw it in the newspaper, you see."
[159] She pointed to a small section of the report, titled "Fatal Accident in Sea Fog."
[160] Last night, the police confirmed the identity of the deceased in the Marigold incident based on a photo carried by the deceased. The person in the photo was identified as Mrs. Leo Kemann. Mrs. Kemann rushed to Marigold upon receiving the news, where she confirmed that the deceased was her brother, Alexander Prichard. Mr. Prichard had recently returned from Siam. He had been away from England for ten years and was preparing to embark on a hiking trip. The inquest into the cause of death will be held tomorrow in Marigold.
[161] Bobby's thoughts drifted back to the face in the photo that for some reason was hard to forget.
[162] "I think I'll have to testify at the inquest," he said.
[163] "How exciting, I'll go to hear you testify."
[164] "I don't find it very exciting," Bobby said, "We just found him, after all."
[165] "Was he dead at the time?"
[166] "No, he wasn't dead yet. He died about fifteen minutes later. He was alone with me."
[167] He paused.
[168] "It was pretty scary," Frankie's keen understanding was something Bob's father didn't have.
[169] "Of course, he couldn't feel anything –"
[170] "Couldn't feel anything?"
[171] "But, you know, he looked so alive. Like a guy like that, just stepping off a cliff in a silly little puff of smoke, it's a pretty lousy way to go."
[172] "I get you, Steve." Frankie's somewhat strange joke again expressed her sympathy and understanding.
[173] "Have you met his sister?" She asked again, quickly.
[174] "No. I went into town for two days to see a friend. He's opening an auto repair shop. You should remember him, Budge Bidden."
[175] "Should I?"
[176] "Of course you should remember him, you must remember the kind old Baz. He was a bit cross-eyed."
[177] Frankie furrowed her brow.
[178] "He had a silly laugh, 'heh heh heh', like this." Bobby continued to help her recall.
[179] Frankie remained furrowed.
[180] "We used to be kids, and he fell down headfirst into the mud, and we had to pull him out by his legs." Bobby continued.
[181] "Oh!" Frankie's mind was suddenly flooded with memories, "I remember now, he stuttered a bit when he talked."
[182] "He still stutters," Bobby said proudly.
[183] "Did he run a chicken farm that went bankrupt?" Frankie asked.
[184] "Yes."
[185] "And then he went to a stock brokerage firm, and they fired him a month later?"
[186] "That's right."
[187] "And they sent him to Australia, and he came back?"
[188] "Yes."
[189] "Bobby," Franky said, "I hope you didn't invest in this risky business."
[190] "I don't have any money to invest," Bobby said.
[191] "Thank goodness," Franky said.
[192] "Of course," Bobby continued, "Baz wanted to find someone with a bit of capital to invest, but it's not as simple as you think."
[193] "When you look around," Franky said, "you wouldn't believe there's any rationality to human beings, but there is."
[194] This statement finally struck a chord with Bobby.
[195] "Listen, Franky," he said, "Baz is a good guy, one of the best."
[196] "They usually are," said Frankie.
[197] "Who's they?"
[198] "Those who went to Australia and came back. How did he get the money to start this business?"
[199] "His aunt or someone passed away and left him a garage that could fit six cars, plus the three rooms above, and his family used a hundred pounds to buy a second-hand car. You'd be surprised at how affordable second-hand cars can be."
[200] "I've bought one before," said Frankie, "it's a painful topic, let's not go there. Why did you leave the navy? They didn't fire you, did they? You're too young for that."
[201] Bobby's face turned red.
[202] "It's my eyes," he said grumpily.
[203] "I remember you've had some issues with your eyes."
[204] "I know, I tried to cope with it. I went abroad for service, you know, where the light was too strong, really bad for the eyes. So, uh, I had to leave."
[205] "That's so cruel," Franky said, looking out the window and muttering under his breath.
[206] There was a significant pause that followed.
[207] "Anyway, it's still pretty embarrassing," Bobbie suddenly spoke up again, "My eyes aren't that bad. They said they wouldn't get worse, I could've continued serving."
[208] "Your eyes look fine," Frankie said.
[209] She gazed straight into the honest brown depths of his eyes.
[210] "So you see," Bobbie said, "I'm planning to invest in Bajee."
[211]Frankie nodded.
[212]A server pushed open the door and said, "Round one of lunch."
[213]“Shall we?” Frankie said.
[214]They walked towards the front buffet.
[215]During the time when the ticket taker might appear, Bobbie strategically averted her gaze for a brief moment.
[216] "We don't want to put too much of a burden on his conscience," he said.
[217] However, Frankie said she didn't expect the ticket collector to have any conscience.
[218] By 5:00 pm, they arrived at Selhurst, where Marquis Bolt would be getting off.
[219] "I'll pick you up," Frankie said.
[220] "Thanks. That would save me from having to carry these dreadful bags for two miles."
[221] He contemptuously kicked his suitcase with one foot.
[222] "It's three miles, not two miles," Frankie said.
[223] "If you take the little path through the golf course, it's two miles."
[224] "That's the one--"
[225] "Yes, that's the path he took."
[226] "Did anyone push him down?" Frankie asked casually as he handed the dressing box to the maid.
[227] "Push him down? Oh, no. How could you even think that?"
[228] "Well, that would make it more interesting, wouldn't it?" Frankie said lazily.

[231] Chapter 4: Inquest into the Cause of Death
[232] The inquest into the cause of death of Alex Pritchard was held the following day. Dr. Thomas testified about the process of discovering the body.
[233] "Were there any signs of life at the time?" the coroner asked.
[234] "Yes, the deceased had a pulse. However, there was no hope of resuscitation. His—"
[235] The doctor's language became highly technical at this point, and the coroner was responsible for helping the jury understand his testimony:
[236] "In simpler terms, the man's spine was broken, right?"
[237] "Yes, you could say that," Dr. Thomas said sadly.
[238] He explained how he left to seek assistance, leaving Bobby to watch over the dying man.
[239] "What is your opinion on the cause of this unfortunate incident, Dr. Thomas?"
[240] "I would say that, in the absence of any evidence about his mental state, the deceased most likely slipped and fell off the cliff. There was fog on the sea, and just at that spot, the path suddenly turned inland. Because of the fog, the deceased may not have noticed the danger and continued walking. In this situation, he would have taken only two more steps to cross the cliff edge."
[241] "Were there any signs of a struggle at the scene? For example, could it have been caused by someone else?"
[242] "I can only say that all the injuries can be explained by the impact with the rocks below, about fifty to sixty feet."
[243] "Is there any possibility of suicide?"
[244] "Yes, it is possible. However, I have no idea whether the man walked over the cliff edge or jumped off on his own."
[245] The next witness called was Robert Jones [1].
[246] Bob explained that he was playing golf with a doctor at the time. He hit a right-handed hook shot that flew towards the sea. It was a bit foggy, making it difficult to see clearly. He thought he heard a shout, and for a moment he wondered if the ball had hit someone walking along the path. However, he still believed the ball couldn't have traveled that far.
[247] "Did you find the ball?"
[248] "Yes, it was about a hundred yards away from the path."
[249] He then described how they went to the next tee to hit the ball, and how he himself hit the ball into the ravine.
[250] The coroner interrupted his testimony, as his statement essentially repeated the doctor's testimony. The coroner then began to question Bob about the shout he heard or thought he heard.
[251] "That was a shout."
[252] "A cry for help?"
[253] "No, it was just a loud shout. I'm not even sure if I actually heard it."
[254] "A scream?"
[255] "Kind of," Bob said gratefully, "like the sound someone might make when unexpectedly hit by a ball."
[256] "Or when they think they're walking along and then they step into thin air?"
[257] "Yes."
[258] After that, Bobbie explained that the man died about five minutes after the doctor left to get help, bringing an end to the agony.
[259] The coroner was eager to wrap up the case.
[260] Mrs. Leo Kimmel was called up.
[261] Bobbie took a sharp breath in, overcome with disappointment. The woman in the photo in the deceased's pocket was completely different from her, where did that face go? Bobbie thought with disgust, photographers were the worst scammers. The photo was clearly taken years ago, but it was still hard to believe that the woman with the alluring eyes in the picture would turn into the yellow-faced, brow-plucked, and hair-dyed woman in front of her. Bobbie suddenly realized that time was a terrifying thing. For example, what would Frankie look like twenty years later? She shuddered involuntarily.
[262] Meanwhile, Amelia Kemm from Paddington St. Leonard's Garden No. 17 is giving testimony.
[263] The deceased was her only brother, Alexander Pritchard. She last saw him the day before the tragedy, when he announced that he was going on a hiking trip in Wales. Her brother had recently returned from the East.
[264] "Is he looking happy and emotionally stable?"
[265] "Oh, that's completely normal. Alex [2] is always cheerful."
[266] "Did you know of any concerns he had?"
[267] "Oh! I'm sure he didn't. He was just thinking about his trip at the time."
[268] "Has he encountered any financial or other difficulties lately?"
[269] "Well, I can't say for sure about that," said Mrs. Kemm, "you see, he had just come back, and I hadn't seen him for ten years. He's not much of a letter writer either. But in London, he took me out to see a play and had lunch with me, and he even gave me a couple of gifts, so I don't think he's short of money, and he always seemed so full of energy and enthusiasm, and I didn't think there would be anything else."
[270] "What kind of profession does your brother have, Mrs. Kemm?"
[271] The lady looked slightly uncomfortable.
[272] "Well, I can't say for sure. He said he was exploring. He's hardly ever in England."
[273] "Do you know of anything that could have led him to commit suicide?"
[274] "Oh, no. And I don't believe he would do something like that. This must have been an accident."
[275] "How do you explain your brother not bringing any luggage with him, not even a backpack?"
[276] "He doesn't like backpacks. He planned to send a parcel every other day. He sent one the day before he left, with his overnight things and a pair of socks, but he wrote the address as Derbyshire [3] instead of Leicestershire [4], so it arrived here today."
[277] "Ah! That clears up one mystery."
[278] Mrs. Kemann continued to explain how people contacted her through the photographer's name on the photo her brother carried. She and her husband came to Marchbold and immediately recognized the deceased as her brother.
[279] At the end of her explanation, she broke down in tears.
[280] The coroner said a few comforting words and sent her down.
[281] He then turned to the jury. Their task was to determine how the man had died. Fortunately, the whole thing seemed quite simple. There were no indications that Mr. Prichard had been worried or depressed or in a state of mind that might have led him to commit suicide. On the contrary, he had been in good health and spirits and eagerly anticipating his holiday. Unfortunately, walking along the cliff edge on that narrow path was dangerous when the sea mist rolled in, and most people would agree that it was time to take some action on that path.
[282] The jury quickly reached a verdict:
[283] "We believe the death was the result of an unfortunate accident, and we would like to add an opinion that, in our view, the town council should immediately take measures to install fencing or barriers along the sea cliff edge where the path runs through the chasm."
[284] The coroner nodded in agreement.
[285] The inquest into the cause of death was concluded.

[291] Chapter Five: The Kemans
[292] About half an hour later, when Bobbie returned to the parsonage, he found that his connection with the death of Alex Pritchard was not yet fully resolved. He heard that the Kemans had come to visit him, and were with his father in the study. Bobbie walked into the study and saw his father bravely searching for a suitable topic to converse with the guests, obviously not very fond of the task.
[293] "Ah!" He breathed a sigh of relief, "Bobbie has come."
[294] Mr. Kemans stood up, extending his hand to the young man. Mr. Kemans was a ruddy-faced, burly man who tried to appear warm and friendly, but his cool and somewhat wavering eyes betrayed that he was not sincere. As for Mrs. Kemans, although some might find her bold and clumsy demeanor attractive, she bore little resemblance to the woman in the early photos, and the melancholy expression she once had was gone. In fact, Bobbie thought carefully, whether anyone else could recognize her from her photo if she hadn't recognized herself seemed to be a question.
[295] "My wife and I came together," Mr. Kemans said, shaking Bobbie's hand tightly, "you know, I have to be with her, Amelia is very upset."
[296] Mrs. Kemans sniffed.

[313] "Yeah," Bobby said. He remembered the tanned face and the deep blue eyes. Alex Prichard was an attractive person, even in his dying moments, and it was strange that he had such a sister and brother-in-law. Bobby felt he deserved better.
[314] "Well, we're very grateful, and we owe you a big favor, really," Mrs. Kemann said.

[316] He was desperate and a bit at a loss.
[317] "We won't forget this," Mr. Kemman said. Bobbie endured another painful handshake and then took Mrs. Kemman's soft, weak hand. His father said goodbye again. Bobbie accompanied the Kemmans to the front door of the house.

[319] "Mostly looking for work," Bobbie said, pausing. "I was in the Navy."
[320] "Life is tough, especially now," Mr. Kemman said, shaking his head. "Well, good luck to you."
[321] "Thank you very much," Bobbie said politely.
[322] He watched them drive away down the overgrown driveway.
[323] Standing there, he began to ponder, various thoughts rushing through his mind. Disordered images. The photograph. The girl with the wide-set eyes and hazy hair. And ten or fifteen years later, the Mrs. Kemann with the heavy makeup, plucked eyebrows, wide-set eyes sunken into a double chin, and dyed reddish-brown hair. All traces of youth and innocence were gone. How sad! Perhaps it was because she married a Mr. Kemann like that, a strong, rough man. If she had married someone else, she might have aged gracefully. A touch of gray at the temples, a smooth, pale face, and two eyes still set wide apart, gazing forward. However, it might also be –
[324] Bobbie sighed and shook his head.
[325] "This is the worst marriage," he said glumly.
[326] "What are you talking about?"

[331] "So to say —"

[371] "Well, it's all the same thing, isn't it?" Frankie said. "I mean, it's not like 'I've always loved Gladys' or 'The will is in the walnut desk' or any other sentimental stuff from a book."
[372] "Do you think it's overreacting to tell them about it?"
[373] "I wouldn't bother with it. It's not an important thing to say."
[374] "I hope you're right," Bobbie said before refocusing on his golf game.
[375] However, the matter did not truly leave him. Although it was a small thing, it still bothered him. He felt a vague sense of unease in his mind about it. He believed Frankie's opinion was both correct and wise, that it was an unimportant thing to say and let it go. But he felt a subtle sense of guilt. Saying that the deceased man had said nothing was not true. It was a trivial mistake, but every time he thought about it, he felt a little uncomfortable.
[376] Eventually, that night, he sat down and wrote a letter to Mr. Kemm.
[377] Dear Mr. Kemm, I just remembered that your brother-in-law actually did say something before he passed away. I believe his exact words were, "Why didn't they call Evans?" I apologize for not mentioning it this morning, but I didn't really pay much attention to it at the time, and I think I just forgot about it.
[378] Yours truly,
[379] Robert Jones
[380] He received a reply the following day:
[381] Dear Mr. Jones (Mr. Keiman wrote), I have received your letter of the sixth. Thank you very much for taking the time to relay my late brother-in-law's final words to me, even though they were indeed insignificant. My wife had hoped that he might have left a message for her. Nevertheless, thank you for your diligence and attention to detail.
[382] Yours faithfully,
[383] Leo Keiman

[385] Chapter Six: The End of the Picnic
[386] The next day, Bobbie received a letter of a different nature:

In fact, I spent fifteen pounds yesterday and got five cars. One Austin, two Morris, and two Rover. They're not roadworthy at the moment, but I think we can get them running. Damn it, they're cars, after all. As long as the buyers can get them home and not break down on the way, that's good enough. I'm thinking of opening next Monday, and I'm counting on you, old chap. Don't let me down, will you? I must say, Aunt Carrie has been very generous. Her neighbor has been rude to her because of her cats, and she's been holding a grudge ever since. I broke his window once, and ever since then, she's given me five pounds at Christmas, and this time is no different.
[388] We're bound to succeed. It's a done deal. I mean, they're cars, after all. You can pick them up for nothing. Paint them up a bit, and those fools will notice. Our business will be a success. Don't forget, next Monday, and I'm counting on you.

[499] While he was thinking back and forth, the problem was solved when he fell asleep unintentionally.

A delightful countryside walk of about twelve miles was exactly what a healthy young man should do. This inevitably reminded him of the famous saying: "Now, I think I've earned my lunch."
[501] "What a fool," Bob thought to himself. "Why walk a long way you don't really want to go to earn your lunch? What's the point? If you enjoy it, it's pure indulgence, if you don't like it, it's stupid."

[504] He felt like a god lying there. The world was at his feet. It was a nice saying, but a good one. He could do anything as long as he tried! Grandiose plans and bold ambitions swept over him.
[505] Then, a wave of drowsiness overcame him, making him feel sleepy again.
[506] He fell asleep...

[511] Chapter 7: A Near-Death Experience
[512] Frankie drove her green Bentley to the curb in front of an old-fashioned large house with the sign "St. Assaf" on the door.
[513] Frankie jumped out of the car, turned around, took out a large bunch of lilies, and then rang the doorbell. A woman wearing a nurse's uniform came to answer the door.
[514] "Can I visit Mr. Jones?" Frankie asked.
[515] The nurse looked at the Bentley and the lilies, then at Frankie, showing a thick interest in her eyes.
[516]"How should I address you?"
[517]"Miss Frances DeWinter."
[518]The nurse was thrilled, thinking her patient should be up by now.
[519]She led Frances upstairs to a room on the second floor.
[520]"Someone's here to see you, Mr. Jones. Who do you think it could be? What a surprise!"
[521] The staff at the sanatorium spoke in this unusually "upbeat" manner.
[522] "Oh my goodness!" exclaimed Bobbie, "It's Frankie!"
[523] "Hello, Bobbie. I brought some ordinary flowers. They're a bit reminiscent of a graveyard, but that's all that was available."
[524] "Oh, Miss Frances, these flowers are lovely. I'll go put them in water."
[525] She left the room.
[526] Frankie sat in a chair that was clearly meant for visitors.
[527] "Well, Bobbie," she said, "What's going on?"
[528] "I knew you'd ask," said Bobbie, "I've become quite the sensation around here. At least eight morphine tablets, they're planning to write me up in The Lancet [1] and the BMJ [2]."
[529] "What's the BMJ?" Frankie interjected.
[530] "British Medical Journal."
[531] "Alright, keep going, give me a few more acronyms."
[532] "Do you know what half a pill is the lethal dose, little girl? I should have died about sixteen times already. I mean, I've heard of people surviving after taking sixteen pills, but eight is still a lot, don't you think? I've become a hero here, they've never had a case like mine before."
[533] "Well, that's great for them. They've got something to talk about with all the other patients."
[534] "Isn't it? Gives them something to gossip about."
[535] The nurse came back into the room, holding a vase of lilies.
[536] "See, nurse, you said it yourself. You've never had a case like mine before, right?" Bobby asked.
[537] "Oh! You shouldn't even be here," the nurse said. "You should be in the cemetery. But they say only good people die young. She chuckled at her own wit and left the room.
[538] "I told you so," Bobby said. "You'll see, I'll be famous all over England."
[539] He continued talking, the self-consciousness he had shown when with Frankie was gone. He went into great detail about his condition, enjoying it and never getting tired of it.
[540] "Enough," Frankie interrupted. "I really don't care about the washing machine. It's like you're the only person who's ever been poisoned."
[541] "It's rare to survive eight morphine overdoses," Bobby explained, "It's really something to behold."
[542] "It must have been a real pain for the person who poisoned you," Frankie said.
[543] "Yes, what a waste of good morphine."
[544] "The poison was in the beer, wasn't it?"
[545] "Yes. You know, they found me sleeping like a corpse and couldn't wake me up. Then they panicked and took me to a farmhouse, called the doctor –"
[546] "I know what happened next." Frankie interjected.
[547] "At first, they thought I intentionally took it. Then, when I told them the whole story, they went to look for the beer bottle and found it right where I threw it. They sent it for analysis, and it was clear there was enough leftover to kill a person."
[548] "Was there any indication of how the morphine got into the bottle?"
[549] "None at all. They went to the bar where I bought the bottle and opened the other bottles, but everything was normal."
[550] "Someone must have put the drug in the beer while you were asleep."
[551] "That's exactly what I thought. I remember the paper on the bottle's cap was not sticking properly."
[552] Frankie nodded thoughtfully.
[553] "Well," she said, "that proves what I said on the train was true."
[554] "What did you say?"
[555] "I said that the man, Prichard, was pushed off the cliff."
[556] "That wasn't on the train, you said it at the station." Bobby said weakly.
[557] "Same thing."
[558] "But why—"
[559] "Dear, it's obvious. Why would someone want you gone? You're not an heir to any fortune."
[560] "Maybe I am. Maybe my unheard-of aunt in New Zealand will leave all the money to me."
[561] "Bullshit. She wouldn't leave you anything if she didn't know you. If she didn't know you, why would she leave it to her fourth son? Besides, times are tough, even for a priest's family. It's unlikely they have four sons. It's clear as day, no one stands to gain from your death, so that's out. Then there's revenge. Did you happen to seduce the daughter of a pharmacist?"
[562] "I don't remember that happening." Bobby said seriously.
[563] "I know. You've slept around so much, it's hard to keep track. But I can say with certainty that you've never seduced anyone."

[566] "Well, I've never seduced the daughter of a pharmacist."
[567] "So, do you know anyone you've made enemies with?"
[568] Bobby shook his head.

[570] "They think it's a crazy person."
[571] "Nonsense. A madman wouldn't be carrying around countless doses of morphine and then putting them in a weird beer bottle. No, someone pushed Preacher off the cliff. A couple of minutes later you showed up, and he thought you saw what he did, so he decided to take you out."
[572] "I don't buy that, Frankie."
[573] "Why not?"
[574] "Well, for starters, I didn't see anything."
[575] "Yeah, but he doesn't know that."
[576] "And if I had seen something, I would have mentioned it at the inquest."
[577] "That's true." Frankie reluctantly agreed.
[578] She pondered for a moment.

[580] Bobby nodded.
[581] "Yeah, I understand what you mean, but it doesn't seem likely, somehow."

[585] "Those ideas seem too far-fetched."
[586] "I think it makes perfect sense. If two unusual things happened in a place like Machiavelli—wait, there's a third one."

[588] "The job offer they gave you. Of course, that was a small thing, but you have to admit, it was also pretty strange. I've never heard of a foreign company specifically looking for mediocre ex-Navy guys."

[591] "Isn't that a bit extreme? And it's also a big risk, isn't it?"
[592] "Oh! Murderers don't care about consequences. The more people they kill, the more they want to kill."
[593] "Like in 'The Third Drop of Blood.'"

[597] "I see you've gone to Dr. Scondrick [4] in your thoughts. That's impossible, because if I saw something, the police would have seen it too."
[598] "Yeah, that's a pretty stupid idea. This is really getting to me, isn't it?"
[599] "That theory does sound appealing to me," Bobbie said. "It makes me feel important. But it's just a theory, after all."
[600] "I'm definitely right," Frankie stood up. "I have to go now. Can I come see you tomorrow?"
[601] "Oh! Come on. The nurses' teasing is just boring. By the way, are you coming straight from London?"
[602] "Darling, I rushed back as soon as I heard about your situation. It's so exciting to have a friend who can be poisoned so romantically."
[603] "I don't know if morphine is really that romantic," Bobby said, recalling something.

[610] "I often see her pictures in the newspaper, but she doesn't look like the photos. And of course, I've seen her driving around before, but I've never seen her up close like this. She's not arrogant, is she?"
[611] "Oh, no!" Bobbie said. "I would never say Frankie is arrogant."
[612] "I told the head nurse she's very approachable. No airs at all, and I told her she's just like you and me."
[613] Bobbie didn't respond, using his silence to strongly oppose the idea. The nurse, disappointed by his lack of response, left the room.
[614] Bobbie was left alone with his thoughts.
[615] He finished his tea and, after pondering Frankie's astonishing deductions, reluctantly decided not to agree with them and began seeking other forms of amusement.
[616] His gaze was drawn to the lilies in the vase. It was so kind of Frankie to bring him these flowers, and they were beautiful, but he wished she had brought him some detective novels instead. He looked over at the table next to him. There was a novel by W. W. Jacobs, a copy of "Gentleman John Halifax" by John Halifax, and last week's "Marblehead Gazette."
[617] Five minutes later, he put the book down. For a mind that had been nourished by "The Third Drop of Blood," "The Great Crime," and "The Strange Adventures of the Florentine Dagger," "Gentleman John Halifax" was not quite up to snuff.
[618] He sighed and picked up last week's "Marblehead Gazette."

[654] [1] The world-renowned medical journal published by British Elsevier Publishing Company, named after the surgical knife "Lancet."
[655] [2] One of the world's four most famous general medical journals.
[656] [3] Refers to George Joseph Smith, a notorious bigamist and serial killer in early 20th century Britain. From 1912 to 1914, he drowned three of his brides with different names in the bathtub, and Smith and Armstrong were both famous British murderers.
[657] [4] A famous forensic detective character created by British crime writer Austin Freeman, a model for scientific crime-solving in detective fiction. The novel "The Singing Bone" (New Star Publishing, published in 2010) is one of his notable works.
[658] [5] The pen name of the British novelist and poet Maria Louise Ramé, who wrote over 40 novels in her lifetime.
[659] [6] A notable work by British novelist and poet Dina Maria Craik, published in 1857.
[660] Chapter Eight: The Mystery of the Photograph
[661] They gazed at each other, trying to adjust to the sudden turn of events.
[662] "It couldn't be anyone else," Bobby said. "He's the only one who had the chance."
[663] "Unless, as we said, there were two photos."
[664] "We both know that's unlikely. If there were two photos, they would have used both to identify the victim, not just one."
[665] "Either way, it's easy to find out," Frankie said. "We can ask the police. For now, let's assume there's only one photo, the one you saw and put back in his pocket. You left him with it, and the police arrived without it, so the only one who could have taken it, then put another photo back in his pocket, is this Mr. Baskerville-French."
[666] Bobbie furrowed her brow, trying to remember.
[667] "He's the kind of guy with no distinguishing features, hard to describe. He had a pleasant voice, like a gentleman. I really didn't pay much attention to him. He said he was new here, looking for a place."

[740] "Great work, Frankie. Yes, he must have just come back from abroad, or just left (his skin is incredibly black, like a hunter who catches large game, he looks like that kind of person), and he can't have any close relatives who know his whereabouts."

[769] Chapter 9: Mr. Baxton-French
[770] Frankie immediately began to act, launching an attack on his father that night.
[771]"Dad," she said, "do you know anyone in the Baxton-French family?"
[772]The Earl of Marchmont was reading a political article and did not hear this question properly.
[773]"It's as much French as it is American," he snapped, "seeing how stupid and silly they are, and how much time and money they waste on their meetings—"
[774]Frankie half-listened to him as he rambled on, the Earl's words flowing like a train on a well-worn track until they reached their destination.
[775]"Baxton-French family," Frankie repeated.
[776] "What about them?" the Earl of Marsham asked.
[777] Frankie didn't know what to ask. She knew her father liked to argue with people, so she started the conversation.
[778] "They're from Yorkshire, aren't they?"
[779] "Nonsense, they're from Hampshire. Of course, there's a branch in Shropshire, and then there's one in Ireland. Which one is your friend from?"
[780] "I'm not sure," Frankie said, implying that she had become friends with someone she barely knew.
[781] "Not sure? What do you mean? You have to be sure."
[782] "Nowadays, people are all over the place," Frankie said.
[783] "Wandering, wandering — they'll just keep wandering. In my time, we'd just ask one question, and we'd know where they were from. He said he was from the Hampshire branch, fine, your grandmother married my distant cousin. So there's the connection."
[784] "That must have been lovely," Frankie said. "It's just that nowadays, there's no time for family trees and geographical research."
[785] "Yeah, you don't have time for anything these days, except drinking those poisonous cocktails."
[786] Lord Marchmont moved his gouty leg and let out a sharp cry of pain, even the home-brewed Bordeaux could not alleviate the suffering.
[787] "Are they wealthy?" Frankie asked.
[788] "The Bassington-French family? Not really. I remember the Shropshire branch still suffering financially due to inheritance tax and such. The Hampshire branch had someone who married a wealthy American heiress."
[789] "Someone from their family came by the other day," Frankie said. "Seemed to be looking for a house."
[790] "What a joke, looking for a house here?"
[791] Frankie thought that was the crux of the matter.
[792] The next day she walked into the office of the real estate agents, Wheeler and Owen.
[793] Mr. Owen rose to greet her. Frankie gave him a polite smile and sat down heavily in a chair.
[794] "What can we do for you, Miss Francis? I suppose you're not here to sell the castle, ha ha!"
[795] "I wish I could," Frankie said. "Actually, I came because I heard a friend of mine came here a few days ago. Mr. Barsham-French, he was looking for a house."
[796] "Ah, yes, there was someone by that name. I remember two lowercase f's."
[797] "That's right," Franky said.
[798] "He was asking about all sorts of small properties, wanting to buy. He had to go back to the city the next day, so he couldn't see many houses, but I figured he wasn't in any rush. [2648] She struggled frantically, twisting her waist and kicking her feet, but it was all for naught. No matter how she fought, she felt herself growing weaker. She heard a buzzing in her ears, felt short of breath, and then lost consciousness…
